Biography of a Celebrity

Darwin (5) 279

— Jifan

After completing the writing about Darwin's theories, this brief biography is also coming to its final section. However, we cannot but briefly mention the daily life of this giant in his later years, otherwise, we would still have regrets about the record of this scientist's life.

"He is an extraordinary scientist, but his daily life is very ordinary." This is what one of his friends often told visitors to Darwin. True, daily life is always determined by the circumstances of the time. It is not necessarily the case that a "genius" has a life that is beyond the ordinary. For example, Darwin's life in his youth, as we see it, although it also revealed his talent, was nothing more than a typical life of an ordinary young man, with no special features. Darwin's later years were even more affected by his ill health, which greatly affected his work. He really suffered all aspects of life - pain and happiness - he was a brave warrior who had really struggled in life.

However, the lives of people living in the same era naturally inevitably share the same color, which is the generality of life! But every individual also often has more or less different characteristics, which is also a fact, because it is due to the special restrictions of personal life. This is also the source of anecdotes about celebrities. Darwin's family life was extremely happy, and he showed his tenderness and love to his wife. In his book "The Expression of the Emotions," he described his meticulous attention to his children. He said that when children cry, he loves to watch the expressions of children when they cry, but he is often softened, and he comforts and kisses them endlessly before they finish crying. He often joined the children, acting as an age-appropriate companion. The children did not treat him as an old man, and played with him, taking it as entertainment. He never uttered angry words to his children throughout his life, and was also very courteous to his servants. He was very hospitable, and treated his guests with utmost courtesy, often sitting around and chatting, fearing that he could not make them fully happy. Among his friends, Huxley was famous for his eloquent conversation, and Darwin loved to hear his witty remarks; Lyell and Hooker often discussed science, often arguing with each other, without reaching a conclusion. However, Darwin said in his memoirs: "Among the friends I have made, perhaps none is more lovable than Hooker," which shows the depth of their feelings.

Darwin loved art, but his hands were clumsy and he could not draw pictures. He loved music, but his ears were not sensitive and he could not distinguish its rhythms. This is probably a unique shortcoming of giants - his life was very regular in his later years. His eating, sleeping, and sitting all had fixed times. But in terms of diet, he sometimes still retained the innocence of his youth. He loved sweets very much, and doctors often forbade it, and his family did not prepare much, but he could not change it.

"Darwin's Yellow Notebook" is one of the treasures of the London Library, a treasure for those who study Darwin's life. However, during Darwin's lifetime, these booklets were often placed on a shelf above a fireplace. That was Darwin's diary for many years, piled together. The records in it mainly focus on work, such as what day he worked, what day he could not work due to illness, in great detail. From this, we can know that he hated illness very much. In addition, the diaries about travel and returning home are also recorded in great detail. When he had insomnia for several nights and felt dizzy, his wife advised him to travel for a few days, but he always refused to listen, or argued with his wife about the date. Even if he made it clear that he would return in seven days, he always returned one or two days earlier. He cherished time so much in his later years. Here, we can also see that if he had not had the pain of illness, his academic success might have exceeded the limits he had already reached.

Another method he used to cherish time was "not doing something a second time." He said that, for example, many experiments, instead of doing them repeatedly, it is better to pay attention to them thoroughly and do them well once, so that the effort is not wasted. Therefore, he regarded every small experiment as sacred, because he was unwilling to waste time. This point is very worthy of our attention.

He also had a way of cherishing time, that is, to work to the limit of his strength, "I think I should stop here—" then stop, otherwise, he would never give up. He also paid great attention to the progress and intensity of his work, and often maintained the pleasure and interest of his work. When he was extremely busy, he often said to himself: "I am doing it out of pleasure, not out of compulsion!" As for his work spirit, it is even beyond the reach of ordinary people. He regarded things that people considered exceptions or trivial as sacred, and never easily let them go. The second is his stubbornness. This stubbornness refers to his discovery of truth. In his old age, he still loved to read, experiment, and be vigilant about himself.

In short, he lived and died in struggle, in the pursuit of truth.

We accept his theory and admire his great personality. We should continue his research and get closer to the truth we are pursuing. (The End)
